<tr style="font-size: 85%;">
<td colspan="3"><b>Warning</b>: <i>Value specified for "<a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_former_country/doc#Location" title="Template:Infobox former country/doc">continent</a>" <a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_former_country/doc#Location" title="Template:Infobox former country/doc">does not comply</a></i></td>

<p>The <b>Russian Empire</b> (also known as <b>Russia</b>) was an <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empire</a> that existed from 1721 until it was overthrown by the short-lived <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">February Revolution in 1917</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empires</a> in world history, stretching over three continents, the Russian Empire was surpassed in landmass only by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> empires. The rise of the Russian Empire happened in association with the decline of neighboring rival powers: the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Swedish Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Persia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. It played a major role in 1812–1814 in defeating <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>'s ambitions to control Europe and expanded to the west and south.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Romanov" title="House of Romanov">House of Romanov</a> ruled the Russian Empire from 1721 until 1762, and its German-descended cadet branch, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Holstein-Gottorp-Romanov" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Holstein-Gottorp-Romanov">House of Holstein-Gottorp-Romanov</a>, ruled from 1762. At the beginning of the 19th century, the Russian Empire extended from the Arctic Ocean in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> in the south, from the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> on the west to the Pacific Ocean, and (until 1867) into <a href="/wiki/Russian_Alaska" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Alaska">Alaska</a> in North America on the east.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> With 125.6 million subjects registered by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire_Census" title="Russian Empire Census">1897 census</a>, it had the third-largest population in the world at the time, after <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing China</a> and India. Like all empires, it included a large disparity in terms of economics, ethnicity, and religion. There were numerous dissident elements, who launched numerous rebellions and assassination attempts; they were closely watched by the <a href="/wiki/Okhrana" title="Okhrana">secret police</a>, with thousands exiled to <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>.</p>
<p>Economically, the empire had a predominately agricultural base, with low productivity on large estates worked by serfs (until they were freed in 1861). The economy slowly industrialized with the help of foreign investments in railways and factories. The land was ruled by a nobility (the <a href="/wiki/Boyars" class="mw-redirect" title="Boyars">boyars</a>) from the 10th through the 17th centuries, and subsequently by an <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_All_Russia" title="Emperor of All Russia">emperor</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Tsar Ivan III</a> (1462–1505) laid the groundwork for the empire that later emerged. He tripled the territory of his state, ended the dominance of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>, renovated the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a>, and laid the foundations of the Russian state. Tsar <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> (1682–1725) fought numerous wars and expanded an already huge empire into a major European power. He moved the capital from <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> to the new model city of <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a>, and led a cultural revolution that replaced some of the traditionalist and medieval social and political mores with a modern, scientific, Europe-oriented, and rationalist system.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> (reigned 1762–1796) presided over a golden age; she expanded the state by conquest, colonization and diplomacy, continuing Peter the Great's policy of modernisation along West European lines. Tsar <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Russia" title="Alexander II of Russia">Alexander II</a> (1855–1881) promoted numerous reforms, most dramatically the emancipation of all 23 million serfs in 1861. His policy in Eastern Europe involved protecting the Orthodox Christians under the rule of the Ottoman Empire. That connection by 1914 led to Russia's entry into the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> on the side of France, Britain, and Serbia, against the German, Austrian and Ottoman empires.</p>
<p>The Russian Empire functioned as an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_of_1905" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolution of 1905">Revolution of 1905</a> and then became a de jure constitutional monarchy. The empire collapsed during the <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">February Revolution of 1917</a>, largely as a result of massive failures in its participation in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Russia" title="History of Russia">History of Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_Russia" title="Territorial changes of Russia">Territorial changes of Russia</a></div>
<p>Though the Empire was only officially proclaimed by Tsar <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter I</a>, following the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nystad" title="Treaty of Nystad">Treaty of Nystad</a> (1721), some historians would argue that it was truly born either when <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III of Russia</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Veliky_Novgorod" title="Veliky Novgorod">Veliky Novgorod</a> or when <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan the Terrible</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Kazan" title="Khanate of Kazan">Khanate of Kazan</a>. According to another point of view, the term <i>Tsardom</i>, which was used after the coronation of Ivan IV in 1547, was already a contemporary Russian word for empire,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> while Peter the Great just replaced it with a Latinized synonym.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much of Russia's expansion occurred in the 17th century, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Siberia" title="History of Siberia">first Russian colonisation of the Pacific</a> in the mid-17th century, the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Polish_War_(1654%E2%80%9367)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Polish War (1654–67)">Russo-Polish War (1654–67)</a> that incorporated left-bank Ukraine, and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_conquest_of_Siberia" title="Russian conquest of Siberia">Russian conquest of Siberia</a>. Poland was divided in the 1790-1815 era, with much of the land and population going to Russia. Most of the 19th century growth came from adding territory in Asia, south of Siberia.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_Russian_Empire" title="Foreign policy of the Russian Empire">Foreign policy of the Russian Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eighteenth_century">Eighteenth century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Eighteenth century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Russia_(1721%E2%80%9396)" title="History of Russia (1721–96)">History of Russia (1721–96)</a></div>
<h4><span id="Peter_the_Great_(1672–1725)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Peter_the_Great_.281672.E2.80.931725.29">Peter the Great (1672–1725)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Peter the Great (1672–1725)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg/220px-Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg/330px-Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg/440px-Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg 2x" data-file-width="894" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_der-Grosse_1838.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> officially renamed the Tsardom of Russia to the Russian Empire in 1721, and became its first emperor. He instituted the <a href="/wiki/Government_reform_of_Peter_the_Great" title="Government reform of Peter the Great">sweeping reforms</a> and oversaw the transformation of Russia into a major European power.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter I</a> the Great (1672–1725) played a major role in introducing Russia to the European state system. However, this vast land had a population of 14 million. Grain yields trailed behind those of agriculture in the West,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> compelling nearly the entire population to farm. Only a small percentage lived in towns. The class of <a href="/wiki/Kholop" title="Kholop">kholops</a>, close to the one of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, remained a major institution in Russia until 1723, when <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter I</a> converted household kholops into house <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>, thus including them in poll taxation. Russian agricultural kholops were formally converted into <a href="/wiki/Serfdom_in_Russia" title="Serfdom in Russia">serfs</a> earlier in 1679.</p>
<p>Peter's first military efforts were directed against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a>. His attention then turned to the North. Peter still lacked a secure northern seaport, except at <a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Archangel</a> on the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a>, but the harbor there was frozen for nine months a year. Access to the Baltic was blocked by Sweden, whose territory enclosed it on three sides. Peter's ambitions for a "window to the sea" led him to make a secret alliance with <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a> in 1699, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> and Denmark against Sweden, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a>. The war ended in 1721 when an exhausted Sweden asked for peace with Russia. Peter acquired four provinces situated south and east of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>. The coveted access to the sea was now secured. There he built Russia's new capital, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, to replace Moscow, which had long been Russia's cultural center. In 1722, he turned on his aspirations as first Russian monarch to increase Russian influence in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> at the expense of the at that time weakened <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid Persians</a>. To do so, he made <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan" title="Astrakhan">Astrakhan</a> the centre of military efforts against Persia, and waged the first full-scale war <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1722-1723)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Persian War (1722-1723)">against them</a> in 1722–23.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peter reorganized his government based on the latest political models of the time, moulding Russia into an <a href="/wiki/Political_absolutism" class="mw-redirect" title="Political absolutism">absolutist</a> state. He replaced the old <i>boyar</i> <a href="/wiki/Duma" title="Duma">Duma</a> (council of nobles) with a nine-member Senate, in effect a supreme council of state. The countryside was also divided into new provinces and districts. Peter told the Senate that its mission was to collect tax revenues. In turn tax revenues tripled over the course of his reign. As part of the government reform, the Orthodox Church was partially incorporated into the country's administrative structure, in effect making it a tool of the state. Peter abolished the patriarchate and replaced it with a collective body, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">Holy Synod</a>, led by a government official. Meanwhile, all vestiges of local self-government were removed. Peter continued and intensified his predecessors' requirement of state service for all nobles.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peter died in 1725, leaving an unsettled succession. After a short reign of his widow <a href="/wiki/Catherine_I_of_Russia" title="Catherine I of Russia">Catherine I</a>, the crown passed to empress <a href="/wiki/Anna_of_Russia" title="Anna of Russia">Anna</a> who slowed down the reforms and led a successful <a href="/wiki/Austro-Russian%E2%80%93Turkish_War_(1735%E2%80%9339)" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Russian–Turkish War (1735–39)">war against the Ottoman Empire</a>, which brought a significant weakening of the Ottoman vassal <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a>, a long-term Russian adversary.</p>
<p>The discontent over the dominant positions of <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Germans" title="Baltic Germans">Baltic Germans</a> in Russian politics brought Peter I's daughter <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Russia" title="Elizabeth of Russia">Elizabeth</a> on the Russian throne. Elizabeth supported the arts, architecture and the sciences (for example with the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Moscow University">Moscow University</a>). However, she did not carry out significant structural reforms. Her reign, which lasted nearly 20 years, is also known for her involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>. It was successful for Russia militarily, but fruitless politically.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Catherine_the_Great_(1762–1796)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Catherine_the_Great_.281762.E2.80.931796.29">Catherine the Great (1762–1796)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Catherine the Great (1762–1796)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russia_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War#Russian_Diplomacy_during_the_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Russia in the American Revolutionary War">Russia in the American Revolutionary War §&#160;Russian Diplomacy during the War</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a> was a German princess who married Peter III, the German heir to the Russian crown. After the death of Empress Elizabeth, she came to power when her coup d'état against her unpopular husband succeeded. She contributed to the resurgence of the Russian nobility that began after the death of Peter the Great. State service was abolished, and Catherine delighted the nobles further by turning over most state functions in the provinces to them.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_(1763,_Tretyakov_gallery).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg/220px-Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg/330px-Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg/440px-Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_%281763%2C_Tretyakov_gallery%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1829" data-file-height="2074" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Profile_portrait_of_Catherine_II_by_Fedor_Rokotov_(1763,_Tretyakov_gallery).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Empress <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a>, who reigned from 1762 to 1796, continued the empire's expansion and modernization. Considering herself an <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutist" class="mw-redirect" title="Enlightened absolutist">enlightened absolutist</a>, she played a key role in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Enlightenment" title="Russian Enlightenment">Russian Enlightenment</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Catherine the Great extended Russian political control over the lands of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>. Her actions included the support of the <a href="/wiki/Targowica_Confederation" title="Targowica Confederation">Targowica Confederation</a>, although the cost of her campaigns, on top of the oppressive social system that required serfs to spend almost all of their time laboring on their owners' land, provoked a major peasant uprising in 1773, after Catherine legalised the selling of serfs separate from land. Inspired by a Cossack named <a href="/wiki/Yemelyan_Pugachev" title="Yemelyan Pugachev">Pugachev</a>, with the emphatic cry of "Hang all the landlords!", the rebels threatened to take Moscow before they were ruthlessly suppressed. Instead of the traditional punishment of being drawn and quartered, Catherine issued secret instructions that the executioner should carry the sentence out quickly and with a minimum of suffering, as part of her effort to introduce compassion into the law.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> She also ordered the public trial of <a href="/wiki/Darya_Nikolayevna_Saltykova" title="Darya Nikolayevna Saltykova">Darya Nikolayevna Saltykova</a>, a member of the highest nobility, on charges of torture and murder. These gestures of compassion garnered Catherine much positive attention from Europe experiencing the Enlightenment age, but the specter of revolution and disorder continued to haunt her and her successors.</p>
<p>In order to ensure continued support from the nobility, which was essential to the survival of her government, Catherine was obliged to strengthen their authority and power at the expense of the serfs and other lower classes. Nevertheless, Catherine realized that serfdom must be ended, going so far in her "Instruction" to say that serfs were "just as good as we are" – a comment the nobility received with disgust. Catherine <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Turkish Wars">successfully waged war</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and advanced Russia's southern boundary to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Then, by plotting with the rulers of <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, she incorporated territories of the Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth during the <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>, pushing the Russian frontier westward into Central Europe. In accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Georgievsk" title="Treaty of Georgievsk">treaty</a> Russia had signed with the Georgians to protect them against any new invasion of their Persian suzerains and further political aspirations, Catherine waged a new war <a href="/wiki/Persian_Expedition_of_1796" title="Persian Expedition of 1796">against Persia</a> in 1796 after they had again invaded Georgia and established rule over it about a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Krtsanisi" title="Battle of Krtsanisi">year prior</a> and expelled the newly established Russian garrisons in the Caucasus. By the time of her death in 1796, Catherine's expansionist policy had turned Russia into a major European power.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> This continued with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I's</a> wresting of Finland from the weakened kingdom of Sweden in 1809 and of <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Bessarabia</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Principality of Moldavia</a>, ceded by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> in 1812.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="State_budget">State budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: State budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg/275px-Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg" width="275" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg/413px-Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg/550px-Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4355" data-file-height="2250" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russia_1771_Sestroretsk_Rouble.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Catherine II Sestroretsk Rouble (1771) is made of solid copper measuring 77 millimetres (<span class="frac nowrap">3<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>100</sub></span>&#160;in) (diameter), 26 millimetres (<span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>50</sub></span>&#160;in) (thickness), and weighs 1.022&#160;kg (2.25&#160;lb). It is the largest copper coin ever issued.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Russia was in a continuous state of financial crisis. While revenue rose from 9 million rubles in 1724 to 40 million in 1794, expenses grew more rapidly, reaching 49 million in 1794. The budget allocated 46 percent to the military, 20 percent to government economic activities, 12 percent to administration, and nine percent for the Imperial Court in St. Petersburg. The deficit required borrowing, primarily from Amsterdam; five percent of the budget was allocated to debt payments. Paper money was issued to pay for expensive wars, thus causing inflation. For its spending, Russia obtained a large and well-equipped army, a very large and complex bureaucracy, and a court that rivaled Paris and London. However the government was living far beyond its means, and 18th century Russia remained "a poor, backward, overwhelmingly agricultural, and illiterate country."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_half_of_the_nineteenth_century">First half of the nineteenth century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: First half of the nineteenth century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Russia_(1796%E2%80%931855)" title="History of Russia (1796–1855)">History of Russia (1796–1855)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, following a dispute with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Tsar Alexander I</a>, launched an <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">invasion of Russia</a> in 1812. The campaign was a catastrophe. Although Napoleon's <a href="/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e" title="Grande Armée">Grande Armée</a> made its way to Moscow, the Russians' <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> strategy prevented the invaders from living off the country. In the bitter <a href="/wiki/Russian_Winter" title="Russian Winter">Russian Winter</a>, thousands of French troops were ambushed and killed by peasant guerrilla fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> As Napoleon's forces retreated, the Russian troops pursued them into Central and Western Europe and to the gates of Paris. After Russia and its allies defeated Napoleon, Alexander became known as the 'saviour of Europe', and he presided over the redrawing of the map of Europe at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> (1815), that ultimately made Alexander the monarch of <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Congress Poland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png/220px-Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png/330px-Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png/440px-Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1596" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Borodino_1812.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Borodino" title="Battle of Borodino">Battle of Borodino</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the Russian Empire would play a leading political role in the next century, thanks to its defeat of Napoleonic France, its retention of serfdom precluded economic progress of any significant degree. As Western European economic growth accelerated during the Industrial Revolution, Russia began to lag ever farther behind, creating new weaknesses for the Empire seeking to play a role as a great power. This status concealed the inefficiency of its government, the isolation of its people, and its economic backwardness. Following the defeat of Napoleon, Alexander I had been ready to discuss constitutional reforms, but though <a href="/wiki/Government_reform_of_Alexander_I" title="Government reform of Alexander I">a few were introduced</a>, no major changes were attempted.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fort_Ross_inside.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fort_Ross_inside.jpg/220px-Fort_Ross_inside.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fort_Ross_inside.jpg/330px-Fort_Ross_inside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fort_Ross_inside.jpg/440px-Fort_Ross_inside.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1021" data-file-height="624" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fort_Ross_inside.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fort_Ross,_California" title="Fort Ross, California">Fort Ross</a>, an early-19th-century outpost of the <a href="/wiki/Russian-American_Company" title="Russian-American Company">Russian-American Company</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sonoma_County,_California" title="Sonoma County, California">Sonoma County, California</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The liberal tsar was replaced by his younger brother, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> (1825–1855), who at the beginning of his reign was confronted with an uprising. The background of this revolt lay in the Napoleonic Wars, when a number of well-educated Russian officers travelled in Europe in the course of military campaigns, where their exposure to the liberalism of Western Europe encouraged them to seek change on their return to <a href="/wiki/Autocratic_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Autocratic Russia">autocratic Russia</a>. The result was the <a href="/wiki/Decembrist_revolt" title="Decembrist revolt">Decembrist revolt</a> (December 1825), the work of a small circle of liberal nobles and army officers who wanted to install Nicholas' brother as a constitutional monarch. But the revolt was easily crushed, leading Nicholas to turn away from the modernization program begun by <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> and champion the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy,_Autocracy,_and_Nationality" title="Orthodoxy, Autocracy, and Nationality">Orthodoxy, Autocracy, and Nationality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The retaliation for the revolt made "December Fourteenth" a day long remembered by later revolutionary movements. In order to repress further revolts, censorship was intensified, including the constant surveillance of schools and universities. Textbooks were strictly regulated by the government. Police spies were planted everywhere. Would-be revolutionaries were sent off to Siberia – under Nicholas I hundreds of thousands were sent to <a href="/wiki/Katorga" title="Katorga">katorga</a> there.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Russian armies liberated allied (since the 1783 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Georgievsk" title="Treaty of Georgievsk">Treaty of Georgievsk</a>) <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar dynasty</a>'s occupation in 1802,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1804%E2%80%9313)" title="Russo-Persian War (1804–13)">Russo-Persian War (1804–13)</a> they clashed with Persia over control and consolidation over Georgia, and also got involved in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_War" title="Caucasian War">Caucasian War</a> against the Caucasian Imamate. The conclusion of the 1804-1813 war with Persia made it irrevocably cede what is now <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, Georgia, and most of <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> to Russia following the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gulistan" title="Treaty of Gulistan">Treaty of Gulistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDowling2014728_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDowling2014728-22">[22]</a></sup> To the south west, Russia attempted to expand at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, using recently acquired Georgia at its base for the Caucasus and Anatolian front. The late 1820s were successful military years. Despite losing almost all recently consolidated territories in the first year of the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1826%E2%80%9328)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Persian War (1826–28)">Russo-Persian War of 1826–28</a>, Russia managed to bring an end to the war with highly favourable terms with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Turkmenchay" title="Treaty of Turkmenchay">Treaty of Turkmenchay</a>, including the official gains of what is now <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/I%C4%9Fd%C4%B1r_Province" title="Iğdır Province">Iğdır Province</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDowling2014729_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDowling2014729-23">[23]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russo-Turkish_wars" title="History of the Russo-Turkish wars">1828-29 Russo-Turkish War</a>, Russia invaded northeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and occupied the strategic Ottoman towns of <a href="/wiki/Erzurum" title="Erzurum">Erzurum</a> and <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCm%C3%BC%C5%9Fhane" title="Gümüşhane">Gümüşhane</a> and, posing as protector and saviour of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox population</a>, received extensive support from the region's <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a>. Following a brief occupation, the Russian imperial army withdrew back into Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The question of Russia's direction had been gaining attention ever since Peter the Great's program of modernization. Some favored imitating Western Europe while others were against this and called for a return to the traditions of the past. The latter path was advocated by <a href="/wiki/Slavophilia" title="Slavophilia">Slavophiles</a>, who held the "decadent" West in contempt. The Slavophiles were opponents of bureaucracy who preferred the collectivism of the medieval Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Obshchina" title="Obshchina">obshchina</a></i> or <i>mir</i> over the <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> of the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein1976_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein1976-25">[25]</a></sup> More extreme social doctrines were elaborated by such Russian radicals on the left as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Herzen" title="Alexander Herzen">Alexander Herzen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>.</p>
<p>Russian tsars crushed two uprisings in their newly acquired Polish territories: the <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a> in 1830 and the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a> in 1863. The Russian autocracy gave the Polish artisans and gentry reason to rebel in 1863 by assailing national core values of language, religion, culture.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The result was the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a>, a massive Polish revolt, which was crushed by massive force. France, Britain and Austria tried to intervene in the crisis but were unable to do so. The Russian patriotic press used the Polish uprising to unify the Russian nation, claiming it was Russia's God-given mission to save Poland and the world.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Poland was punished by losing its distinctive political and judicial rights, with Russianization imposed on its schools and courts.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_half_of_the_nineteenth_century">Second half of the nineteenth century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Second half of the nineteenth century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Russia_(1855%E2%80%9392)" title="History of Russia (1855–92)">History of Russia (1855–92)</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="Russia–United Kingdom relations">Russia–United Kingdom relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="clear:both">
<div class="thumbinner">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow: auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/3666px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg" width="3666" height="175" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/5510px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/7332px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="12569" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Romanov_Flag.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Romanov_Flag.svg/220px-Romanov_Flag.svg.png" width="220" height="147" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Romanov_Flag.svg/330px-Romanov_Flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Romanov_Flag.svg/440px-Romanov_Flag.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Flag of the Russian Empire for "Celebrations" from 1858 to 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvnb.7CBonnell.7Cp.3D92_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb.7CBonnell.7Cp.3D92-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harvnb.7CCondee.7Cp.3D49_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb.7CCondee.7Cp.3D49-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Museum_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Museum-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-crwflags_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crwflags-32">[32]</a></sup> It was not as popular as Peter the Great's tricolour, the white-blue-red flag, which was adopted as the official flag in 1883, officialised by the Tsar in 1896; however, it had been used as a <i>de facto</i> flag to represent Russia since the end of the 17th century.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_(1858%E2%80%931917).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg/220px-Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png" width="220" height="147" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg/330px-Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg/440px-Imperial_Standard_of_the_Emperor_of_Russia_%281858%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Imperial Standard of the Tsar, used from 1858 to 1917. Previous versions of the black eagle on gold background were used as far back as Peter the Great's time.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sevastopol.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Sevastopol.jpg/220px-Sevastopol.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Sevastopol.jpg/330px-Sevastopol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Sevastopol.jpg/440px-Sevastopol.jpg 2x" data-file-width="806" data-file-height="550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sevastopol.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The eleven-month <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1854%E2%80%9355)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1854–55)">siege</a> of a Russian naval base at <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1854-55 Russia lost to Britain, France and Turkey in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>. It was fought primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimean peninsula</a>, and to a lesser extent in the Baltic. Since playing a major role in the defeat of Napoleon, Russia had been regarded as militarily invincible, but, once opposed against a coalition of the great powers of Europe, the reverses it suffered on land and sea exposed the decay and weakness of Tsar Nicholas' regime.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Russia" title="Alexander II of Russia">Tsar Alexander II</a> ascended the throne in 1855, desire for reform was widespread. A growing humanitarian movement attacked <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a> as inefficient. In 1859, there were more than 23 million serfs in usually poor living conditions. Alexander II decided to abolish serfdom from above, with ample provision for the landowners, rather than wait for it to be abolished from below in a revolutionary way that would hurt the landowners.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_reform_of_1861" title="Emancipation reform of 1861">emancipation reform of 1861</a> that freed the serfs was the single most important event in 19th-century Russian history. It was the beginning of the end for the landed aristocracy's monopoly of power. Further reforms of 1860s included socio-economic reforms which would clearify the position of the Russian government in the field of property rights and their protection.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Emancipation brought a supply of free labour to the cities, industry was stimulated, and the middle class grew in number and influence. However, instead of receiving their lands as a gift, the freed peasants had to pay a special tax for what amounted to their lifetime to the government, which in turn paid the landlords a generous price for the land that they had lost. In numerous cases the peasants ended up with the smallest amount of land. All the property turned over to the peasants was owned collectively by the <i>mir</i>, the village community, which divided the land among the peasants and supervised the various holdings. Although serfdom was abolished, since its abolition was achieved on terms unfavourable to the peasants, revolutionary tensions were not abated, despite Alexander II's intentions. Revolutionaries believed that the newly freed serfs were merely being sold into <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage slavery</a> in the onset of the industrial revolution, and that the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> had effectively replaced landowners.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg/260px-KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg" width="260" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg/390px-KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg/520px-KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2529" data-file-height="1389" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KarazinNN_VstRusVoyskGRM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russian troops taking <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> (8 June 1868)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander II obtained <a href="/wiki/Outer_Manchuria" title="Outer Manchuria">Outer Manchuria</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing China</a> between 1858–1860 and sold the last territories of <a href="/wiki/Russian_America" title="Russian America">Russian America</a>, Alaska, to the USA in 1867.</p>
<p>In the late 1870s Russia and the Ottoman Empire again clashed in the Balkans. From 1875 to 1877, the Balkan crisis intensified with rebellions against Ottoman rule by various Slavic nationalities, which the Ottoman Turks dominated since the 16th century. This was seen as a political risk in Russia, which similarly suppressed its Muslims in Central Asia and Caucasia. Russian nationalist opinion became a major domestic factor in its support for liberating Balkan Christians from Ottoman rule and making Bulgaria and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> independent. In early 1877, Russia intervened on behalf of Serbian and Russian volunteer forces in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%9378)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877–78)">Russo-Turkish War (1877–78)</a>. Within one year, Russian troops were nearing <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and the Ottomans surrendered. Russia's nationalist diplomats and generals persuaded Alexander II to force the Ottomans to sign the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Stefano" title="Treaty of San Stefano">Treaty of San Stefano</a> in March 1878, creating an enlarged, independent Bulgaria that stretched into the southwestern Balkans. When Britain threatened to declare war over the terms of the Treaty of San Stefano, an exhausted Russia backed down. At the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> in July 1878, Russia agreed to the creation of a smaller Bulgaria, as an autonomous principality inside the Ottoman Empire. As a result, <a href="/wiki/Pan-Slavism" title="Pan-Slavism">Pan-Slavists</a> were left with a legacy of bitterness against Austria-Hungary and Germany for failing to back Russia. The disappointment at the results of the war stimulated revolutionary tensions in the country. However, he helped <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, Romania and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> to gain independence from and strengthen themselves against the Ottomans.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg/220px-Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg/330px-Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg/440px-Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2080" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahvat_grivickogo_reduta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Capturing of the Turkish redoubt during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Plevna" title="Siege of Plevna">Siege of Plevna</a> (1877)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another significant result of the 1877–78 Russo-Turkish War in Russia's favour was the acquisition from the Ottomans of the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Batumi" title="Batumi">Batum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ardahan" title="Ardahan">Ardahan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kars" title="Kars">Kars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a>, which were transformed into the militarily administered regions of <a href="/wiki/Batum_Oblast" title="Batum Oblast">Batum Oblast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kars_Oblast" title="Kars Oblast">Kars Oblast</a>. To replace Muslim refugees who had fled across the new frontier into Ottoman territory the Russian authorities settled large numbers of Christians from an ethnically diverse range of communities in Kars Oblast, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Greeks" title="Caucasus Greeks">Caucasus Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, each of whom hoped to achieve protection and advance their own regional ambitions on the back of the Russian Empire.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexander_III">Alexander III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Alexander III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1881 Alexander II was assassinated by the <a href="/wiki/Narodnaya_Volya" title="Narodnaya Volya">Narodnaya Volya</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nihilist_movement" title="Nihilist movement">Nihilist</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_designated_terrorist_groups" title="List of designated terrorist groups">terrorist organization</a>. The throne passed to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Russia" title="Alexander III of Russia">Alexander III</a> (1881–1894), a reactionary who revived the maxim of "Orthodoxy, Autocracy, and Nationality" of Nicholas I. A committed Slavophile, Alexander III believed that Russia could be saved from turmoil only by shutting itself off from the subversive influences of Western Europe. During his reign Russia declared the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Russian_Alliance" title="Franco-Russian Alliance">Franco-Russian Alliance</a> to contain the growing power of Germany, completed the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Central_Asia#Russian_expansion_into_Central_Asia_.2819th_century.29" title="History of Central Asia">conquest of Central Asia</a> and demanded important territorial and commercial concessions from the Qing. The tsar's most influential adviser was <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Pobedonostsev" title="Konstantin Pobedonostsev">Konstantin Pobedonostsev</a>, tutor to Alexander III and his son Nicholas, and procurator of the Holy Synod from 1880 to 1895. He taught his royal pupils to fear freedom of speech and press, as well as disliking democracy, constitutions, and the parliamentary system. Under Pobedonostsev, revolutionaries were persecuted and a policy of <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> was carried out throughout the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Game">Great Game</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Great Game">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The movement south toward Afghanistan and India alarmed the British, who ignored Russia's quest for a warm-water port and worked to block its advance in what observers called <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">The Great Game</a>. Both nations avoided escalating the tensions into a war, and they became allies in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_twentieth_century">Early twentieth century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early twentieth century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Russia_(1892%E2%80%931917)" title="History of Russia (1892–1917)">History of Russia (1892–1917)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_(1914%E2%80%931917).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_%281914%E2%80%931917%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Russian_Empire_for_private_use_(1914%E2%80%931917).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 1914, the white-blue-red tricolour with a canton of the imperial arms was introduced by an imperial decree on 19 November 1914. It was the Tsar's private Flag and Peter the Great's tricolour continued being the official Flag of Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvnb.7CBonnell.7Cp.3D92_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb.7CBonnell.7Cp.3D92-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harvnb.7CCondee.7Cp.3D49_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb.7CCondee.7Cp.3D49-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Museum_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Museum-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-crwflags_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crwflags-32">[32]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1894, Alexander III was succeeded by his son, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II</a>, who was committed to retaining the autocracy that his father had left him. Nicholas II proved ineffective as a ruler and in the end his dynasty was overthrown by revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> began to show significant influence in Russia, but the country remained rural and poor. The liberal elements among industrial capitalists and nobility believed in peaceful social reform and a constitutional monarchy, forming the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Democratic_Party" title="Constitutional Democratic Party">Constitutional Democratic Party</a> or <i>Kadets</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the left the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Revolutionary_Party" title="Socialist Revolutionary Party">Socialist Revolutionary Party</a> (SRs) incorporated the Narodnik tradition and advocated the distribution of land among those who actually worked it — the peasants. Another radical group was the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party" title="Russian Social Democratic Labour Party">Russian Social Democratic Labour Party</a>, exponents of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> in Russia. The Social Democrats differed from the SRs in that they believed a revolution must rely on urban workers, not the peasantry.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1903, at the <a href="/wiki/2nd_Congress_of_the_Russian_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party" title="2nd Congress of the Russian Social Democratic Labour Party">2nd Congress of the Russian Social Democratic Labour Party</a> in London, the party split into two wings: the gradualist <a href="/wiki/Mensheviks" title="Mensheviks">Mensheviks</a> and the more radical <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a>. The Mensheviks believed that the Russian working class was insufficiently developed and that socialism could be achieved only after a period of bourgeois democratic rule. They thus tended to ally themselves with the forces of bourgeois liberalism. The Bolsheviks, under <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, supported the idea of forming a small elite of professional revolutionists, subject to strong party discipline, to act as the vanguard of the proletariat in order to seize power by force.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> (1904–1905) was a major blow to the Tsarist regime and further increased the potential for unrest. In January 1905, an incident known as "<a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1905)" title="Bloody Sunday (1905)">Bloody Sunday</a>" occurred when Father <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Gapon" title="Georgy Gapon">Georgy Gapon</a> led an enormous crowd to the <a href="/wiki/Winter_Palace" title="Winter Palace">Winter Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> to present a petition to the Tsar. When the procession reached the palace, soldiers opened fire on the crowd, killing hundreds. The Russian masses were so furious over the massacre that a general strike was declared demanding a democratic republic. This marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_of_1905" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolution of 1905">Revolution of 1905</a>. <a href="/wiki/Soviet_(council)" title="Soviet (council)">Soviets</a> (councils of workers) appeared in most cities to direct revolutionary activity. Russia was paralyzed, and the government was desperate.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:512px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Moscow_clad_in_snow_-_Moscou_sur_la_neige_-_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BC_%D1%83%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B5_-_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B3%D1%83_%281908%29%2C_noaudio.ogv/512px--Moscow_clad_in_snow_-_Moscou_sur_la_neige_-_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B6%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BC_%D1%83%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B5_-_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0_%D0%B2_%D1%81%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B3%D1%83_%281908%29%2C_noaudio.ogv.jpg&quot;
